      <p> Working down the list in the table, determine the dollar % usage for each item. This row exhibits the behavior of the cumulative distribution function, where the change from one category to the next is determined. Rank the items according to their dollar % usage in three classes: A = very important, B = moderately important, and C = least important. </p>
      <p> The <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/ABClass.htm" target="new">ABC Inventory Classification</a> JavaScript constructs an empirical cumulative distribution function (ECDF) as a measuring tool and decision procedure for the ABC inventory classification. The following figure depicts the classification based upon the ECDF of the numerical example: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <a href="ABC.GIF"><img alt="ABC inventory classification" src="ABC.GIF" width="174" height="189" border="0"></a> 
       <br>
       <p>ABC inventory classification<br> <b><font color="#dc143c">Click on the image to enlarge it and THEN print it</font></b> </p>
      </center> 
      <p> </p>
      <p> Even with this information, determination of the boundary between categories of items is often subjective. For our numerical example, Class A-items require very tight inventory control, which is more accurate forecasting, better record-keeping, lower inventory levels; whereas Class C-items tend to have less control. </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>The Impacts of the ABC Classification on Managerial Policies and Decisions</b></font> </p>
      <p> Policies and decisions that might be based on ABC classification include the following : </p>
      <ul> 
       <li>Purchasing resources expended should be much higher for A-items than for C-items. </li>
       <li> Physical inventory control should be tighter for A-items; perhaps they belong in more secure area, with the accuracy of their records being verified more frequently. </li>
       <li> Forecasting A-items may warrant more care than forecasting other items. <p> Better forecasting, physical control, supplier reliability, and an ultimate reduction in safety stock and inventory investment can all result from ABC analysis. </p><p> </p></li>
       <li> Inventory systems require accurate records. Without them, managers cannot make precise decisions about ordering, scheduling and shipping. </li>
       <li> To ensure accuracy, incoming and outgoing record keeping must be good, as must be stockroom security. <p> Well-organized inventory storage will have limited access, good housekeeping, and storage areas that hold fixed amounts of inventory. Bins, shelf space, and parts will be labeled accurately. </p><p> </p></li>
       <li> Cycle counting: Even though an organization may have gone to substantial efforts to maintain accurate inventory records, these records must be verified through continuing audits - are known as cycle counting. Most cycle counting procedures are established so that some of each classification is counted each day. A-items will be counted frequently, perhaps once a month. B-items will be counted less frequently, perhaps once a quarter. C- items will be counted even less frequently, perhaps once every 6 months. Cycle counting also has the following advantages: Eliminating the shutdown and interruption of production necessary of annual physical inventories. Eliminating annual inventory adjustments. Providing professional personnel to audit the accuracy of inventory. Allowing the cause of the errors to be identified and remedial action to be taken. Maintaining accurate inventory records. </li>
      </ul> 
      <p> You might like to use the <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/ABClass.htm" target="new">ABC Inventory Classification</a> JavaScript also for checking your hand computation. </p>
      <p> <b>Further Reading</b><br> <font size="-2" face="Bookman Old Style"> Koch R., <i>The 80/20 Principle: The Secret to Success by Achieving More with Less</i>, Doubleday, 1999.<br> </font> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <hr> 
      <p> <a name="rcostbenitexam"> </a></p>
      <h3><a name="rcostbenitexam"><font color="#DC143C">Cost/Benefit Analysis: Economic Quantity</font></a></h3>
      <a name="rcostbenitexam"> </a> Cost-benefit analysis is a process of finding a good answer to the following question: Given the decision-maker's assessment of costs and benefits, which choice should be recommended? The cost-benefit analysis involves the following general steps: Specify a list of all possible courses of actions. Assign a value (positive or negative) to the outcome for each action, and determine the probability of each outcome. Compute the expected outcome for each action. Take the action that has the best-expected outcome. 
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Economic Quantity Determination Application:</b></font> The cost-benefit analysis is often used in economics for the optimal production strategy. Costs are the main concern, since every additional unit adds to total costs. After start-up production cost, the marginal cost of producing another unit is usually constant or rising as the total number of unit increases. Each additional product tends to cost as much or more than the last one. At some point, the additional costs of an extra product will outweigh the additional benefits. </p>
      <p> The best strategy can be found diagrammatically by plotting both benefits and costs on the horizontal axis and choosing the point where the distance between them is the greatest, as shown in the following figure: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <img src="A.GIF"> 
      </center> 
      <p> Alternatively, one may plot net profit and find the optimal quantity where it is at its maximum as depicted by the following figure: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <img src="B.GIF"> 
      </center> 
      <p> Finally, one may plot the marginal benefit and marginal cost curves and choosing the point where they cross, as shown in the following figure: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <img src="C.GIF"> 
      </center> 
      <p> Notice that, since "net gains" are defined as (benefits-costs), at the maximum point its derivative vanishes; therefore, the slope of the net gains function is zero at the optimal quantity. Therefore, to determine the maximum distance between two curves, the focus is on the incremental or marginal change of one curve relative to another. If the marginal benefit from producing one more unit is larger than the marginal cost, producing more is a good strategy. If the marginal benefit from producing one more product is smaller than the additional cost, producing more is a bad strategy. At the optimum point, the additional benefit will just offset the marginal cost; therefore, there is no change in net gains; i.e., the optimal quantity is where its </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center>
        Marginal benefit = Marginal cost 
      </center> 
      <p> </p>
      <p> You might like to use <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/QuadReg.htm" target="new">Quadratic Regression</a> JavaScript to estimate the cost and the benefit functions based on a given data set. </p>
      <p> <b>Further Reading:</b><br> <font size="-2" face="Bookman Old Style"> Brealey R., and S. Myers, <i>Principles of Corporate Finance</i>, McGraw, 2002.<br> </font> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <hr> 
      <p> <a name="rinventcontrol1"> </a></p>
      <h3><a name="rinventcontrol1"><font color="#DC143C">Introduction</font></a></h3>
      <a name="rinventcontrol1"> </a> Inventory control is concerned with minimizing the total cost of inventory. In the U.K. the term often used is stock control. The three main factors in inventory control decision-making process are: 
      <ul> 
       <li> The cost of holding the stock; e.g., based on the interest rate. </li>
       <li> The cost of placing an order; e.g., for raw material stocks, or the set-up cost of production. </li>
       <li> The cost of shortage; i.e., what is lost if the stock is insufficient to meet all demand. </li>
      </ul> The third element is the most difficult to measure and is often handled by establishing a "service level" policy; e. g, a certain percentage of demand will be met from stock without delay. 
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Production systems and Inventory Control:</b></font> In a production process, it is expected to obtain the minimum levels of work-in-process (WIP), possible, satisfying its demands and due dates. Working under these conditions, lead times, inventory levels and processing costs can be reduced. However, the stochastic nature of production, i.e. the arrival of demands and the uncertainty of a machine failure produce inevitable increases of WIP levels. For these and other reasons, many new heuristic production control policies have been developed, introduced and applied in order to control production in existing plants. </p>
      <p> Production control systems are commonly divided into push and pull systems. In push systems, raw materials are introduced in the line and are pushed from the first to the last work station. Production is determined by forecasts in a production-planning center. One of the best-known push systems is material requirement planning (MRP) and manufacturing resources planning (MRPII), both developed in western countries. Meanwhile, in pull systems production is generated by actual demands. Demands work as a signal, which authorizes a station to produce. The most well-known pull systems are Just in time (JIT) and Kanban developed in Japan. </p>
      <p> Comparing what both systems accomplish, push systems are inherently due-date driven and control release rate, observing WIP levels. Meanwhile, pull systems are inherently rate driven and control WIP level, observing throughput. </p>
      <p> Both push and pull systems offer different advantages. Therefore, new systems have been introduced that adopts advantages of each, as a result obtaining hybrid (push-pull) control policies. The constant work in process and the two-boundary control are the best know hybrid systems with a push-pull interface. In both systems, the last station provides an authorization signal to the first one in order to start production, and internally production in pushed from one station to another until the end of the line as finished good inventory. </p>
      <p> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/simulation/sim.htm" target="new">Simulation models</a> are tools developed to observe systems behavior. They are used to examine different scenarios allowing evaluating the performance measure for deciding on the best policy. </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Supply Chain Networks and Inventory Control:</b></font> A supply chain is a network of facilities that procure raw materials, transform them into intermediate goods and then final products, and deliver the products to customers through a distribution system. To achieve an <b>integrated supply chain management</b>, one must have a standard description of management processes, a framework of relationships among the standard processes, standard metrics to measure process performance, management practices that produce best-in-class performance, and a standard alignment to software features and functionality, together with a users? friendly computer-assisted tools. </p>
      <p> Highly effective coordination, dynamic collaborative and strategic alliance relationships, and efficient supply chain networks are the key factors by which corporations survive and succeed in today's competitive marketplace. Thus all existing supply chain management models rightly focus on inventory control policies and their coordinating with delivery scheduling decisions. </p>
      <p> Inventory control decisions are both problem and opportunity for at least three parties Production, Marking, and Accounting departments. Inventory control decision-making has an enormous impact on the productivity and performance of many organizations, because it handles the total flow of materials. Proper inventory control can minimize stock out, thereby reducing capital of an organization. It also enables an organization to purchase or produce a product in economic quantity, thus minimizing the overall cost of the product. </p>
      <p> <b>Further Readings:</b><br> <font size="-2" face="Bookman Old Style"> Hopp W., and M. Spearman, <i>Factory Physics</i> Examines operating policies and strategic objectives within a factory.<br> Louis R., <i>Integrating Kanban With Mrpii: Automating a Pull System for Enhanced Jit Inventory Management</i>, Productivity Press Inc, 1997. It describes an automated kanban principle that integrates MRP into a powerful lean manufacturing system that substantially lowers inventory levels and significantly eliminates non-value-adding actions. <br> </font> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <hr> 
      <p> <a name="rinventcontrol"> </a></p>
      <h3><a name="rinventcontrol"><font color="#DC143C">Economic Order Quantity (EOR) and Economic Production Quantity (EPQ)</font></a></h3>
      <a name="rinventcontrol"> </a> Inventories are, e.g., idle goods in storage, raw materials waiting to be used, in-process materials, finished goods, individuals. A good inventory model allows us to: 
      <p> </p>
      <ul> 
       <li>smooth out time gap between supply and demand; e.g., supply of corn.<br> </li>
       <li>contribute to lower production costs; e.g., produce in bulk<br> </li>
       <li>provide a way of "storing" labor; e.g., make more now, free up labor later<br> </li>
       <li>provide quick customer service; e.g., convenience. </li>
      </ul> 
      <p> For every type of inventory models, the decision maker is concerned with the main question: When should a replenishment order be placed? One may review stock levels at a fixed interval or re-order when the stock falls to a predetermined level; e. g., a fixed safety stock level. </p>
      <p>&nbsp;</p> 
      <table width="640" border="0" align="center" cellpadding="4"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr valign="top"> 
         <td colspan="2"> 
          <div align="center">

         <td width="20%"><font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">Lead time: L</font></td> 
         <td width="80%"><font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">It is the time interval between when an order is placed and when the inventory is replenished.</font></td> 
        </tr> 
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p> The widely used deterministic and probabilistic models are presented in the following sections. </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>The Classical EOQ Model:</b></font> This is the simplest model constructed based on the conditions that goods arrive the same day they are ordered and no shortages allowed. Clearly, one must reorder when inventory reaches 0, or considering lead time L<br> </p>
      <p> The following figure shows the change of the inventory level with time: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <img src="InvenClass.gif"> 
      </center> 
      <p> The figure shows time on the horizontal axis and inventory level on the vertical axis. We begin at time 0 with an order arriving. The amount of the order is the lot size, Q. The lot is delivered all at one time causing the inventory to shoot from 0 to Q instantaneously. Material is withdrawn from inventory at a constant demand rate, x, measured in units per time. After the inventory is depleted, the time for another order of size Q arrives, and the cycle repeats. </p>
      <p> The inventory pattern shown in the figure is obviously an abstraction of reality in that we expect no real system to operate exactly as shown. The abstraction does provide an estimate of the optimum lot size, called the economic order quantity (EOQ), and related quantities. We consider alternatives to those assumptions later on these pages. </p>
      <p> </p>
      <table width="300" border="0" align="center" cellpadding="4"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <td width="35%" height="26"><font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2"></font></td> 
         <td width="30%" height="26"> 
          <div align="center">
           <font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">Ordering</font>
          </div> </td> 
         <td width="5%" height="26">&nbsp;</td> 
         <td width="30%" height="26"> 
          <div align="center">
           <font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">Holding</font>
          </div> </td> 
        </tr>

         <td width="35%"> 
          <div align="right">
           <font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">Total Cost =</font>
          </div> </td> 
         <td width="30%"> 
          <div align="center">
           <font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">C<font><sub>1</sub></font>x/Q</font>
          </div> </td> 
         <td width="5%">+</td> 
         <td width="30%"> 
          <div align="center">
           <font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">C<font><sub>2</sub></font>/(2Q)</font>
          </div> </td> 
        </tr> 
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p> The Optimal Ordering Quantity = Q* = (2xC<font><sub>1</sub></font>/C<font><sub>2</sub></font>) <font><sup>1/2</sup></font>, therefore, <br>The Optimal Reordering Cycle = T* = [2C<font><sub>1</sub></font>/(xC<font><sub>2</sub></font>)]<font><sup>1/2</sup></font> </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C">Numerical Example 1:</font> Suppose your office uses 1200 boxes of typing paper each year. You are to determine the quantity to be ordered, and how often to order it. The data to consider are the demand rate x = 1200 boxes per year; the ordering cost C<font><sub>1</sub></font> = $5 per order; holding cost C<font><sub>2</sub></font> = $1.20 per box, per year. </p>
      <p> The optimal ordering quantity is Q* = 100 boxes, this gives number of orders = 1200/100 = 12, i.e., 12 orders per year, or once a month. </p>
      <p> Notice that one may incorporate the Lead Time (L), that is the time interval between when an order is placed and when the inventory is replenished. </p>
      <p> <br> </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Models with Shortages:</b></font> When a customer seeks the product and finds the inventory empty, the demand can be satisfied later when the product becomes available. Often the customer receives some discount which is included in the backorder cost. </p>
      <p> A model with backorders is illustrated in the following figure: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <img src="InvShort.gif"> 
      </center> 
      <p> In this model, shortages are allowed some time before replenishment. Regarding the response of a customer to the unavailable item, the customer will accept later delivery which is called a backorder. There are two additional costs in this model; namely, the shortage cost (C<font><sub>3</sub></font>), and the backorder cost (C<font><sub>4</sub></font>). </p>
      <p> Since replenishments are instantaneous, backordered items are delivered at the time of replenishment and these items do not remain in inventory. Backorders are as a negative inventory; so the minimum inventory is a negative number; therefore the difference between the minimum and maximum inventory is the lot size. </p>
      <p> </p>
      <table width="550" border="0" align="center" cellpadding="3"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <td><font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2"></font></td> 
         <td> 
          <div align="center">
           <font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">Ordering</font>
          </div> </td> 
         <td width="5%">&nbsp;</td> 
         <td> 
          <div align="center">
           <font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">Holding</font>

          <div align="center">
           <font size="2" face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif">Shortage + Backorder</font>
          </div> </td> 
        </tr>

         <td>

           <font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">Total Cost =</font>

         <td> 
          <div align="center">
           <font size="2" face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif">(Q-S)<font><sup>2</sup></font>C<font><sub>2</sub></font>/(2Q)</font>
          </div> </td> 
         <td width="5%">+</td>

          <div align="center">
           <font size="2" face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif">xSC<font><sub>3</sub></font>/Q + S<font><sup>2</sup></font>C<font><sub>4</sub></font>/(2Q)</font>
          </div> </td> 
        </tr> 
       </tbody>

      <p> If xC<font><sub>3</sub></font> <font><sup>2</sup></font> &lt; 2C<font><sub>1</sub></font>C<font><sub>2</sub></font>, then </p>
      <p> Q* = M/(C<font><sub>2</sub></font>C<font><sub>4</sub></font>), and S* = M/(C<font><sub>2</sub></font>C<font><sub>4</sub></font> +C<font><sub>4</sub></font><font><sup>2</sup></font>) - (xC<font><sub>3</sub></font>)/(C<font><sub>2</sub></font> + C<font><sub>4</sub></font>), where,<br> M = {xC<font><sub>2</sub></font>C<font><sub>4</sub></font>[2C<font><sub>1</sub></font>(C<font><sub>2</sub></font> + C<font><sub>4</sub></font>) - C<font><sub>3</sub></font><font><sup>2</sup></font>]}<font><sup>1/2</sup></font> </p>
      <p> Otherwise, </p>
      <p> Q* = (2xC<font><sub>1</sub></font>/C<font><sub>2</sub></font>)<font><sup>1/2</sup></font>, with S* = 0. </p>
      <p> However, if shortage cost C<font><sub>3</sub></font> = 0, the above optimal decision values will reduce to: </p>
      <p> Q* = [2xC<font><sub>1</sub></font>(C<font><sub>2</sub></font> + C<font><sub>4</sub></font>)/(C<font><sub>2</sub></font>C<font><sub>4</sub></font>)]<font><sup>1/2</sup></font>, and , S* = [2xC<font><sub>1</sub></font>C<font><sub>2</sub></font>/(C<font><sub>2</sub></font>C<font><sub>4</sub></font> + C<font><sub>4</sub></font><font><sup>2</sup></font>)]<font><sup>1/2</sup></font> </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C">Numerical Example 2:</font> Given C<font><sub>3</sub></font> = 0, and C<font><sub>4</sub></font> = 2 C<font><sub>2</sub></font>, would you choose this model? Since S* = Q*/3 under this condition, the answer is, a surprising "Yes". One third of orders must be back-ordered. </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C">Numerical Example 3:</font> Consider the numerical example no. 1 with shortage cost of C<font><sub>4</sub></font> = $2.40 per unit per year. </p>
      <p> The optimal decision is to order Q* = 122 units, allowing shortage of level S = 81.5 units. </p>
      <p> <br> </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Production and Consumption Model:</b></font> The model with finite replenishments is illustrated in the following figure: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <img src="InvProdcon.gif"> 
      </center> 
      <p> Rather than the lot arrives instantaneously, the lot is assumed to arrive continuously at a production rate K. This situation arises when a production process feeds the inventory and the process operates at the rate K greater than the demand rate x. </p>
      <p> The maximum inventory level never reaches Q because material is withdrawn at the same time it is being produced. Production takes place at the beginning of the cycle. At the end of production period, the inventory is drawn down at the demand rate x until it reaches 0 at the end of the cycle. </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <table width="372" border="0" align="center" cellpadding="4"> 
       <tbody>

         <td width="35%"><font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2"></font></td>

         <td width="30%"> 
          <div align="center">
           <font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">xC<font><sub>1</sub></font>/Q</font>

         <td width="5%">+</td> 
         <td width="30%"> 
          <div align="center">
           <font face="Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif" size="2">(K-x)QC<font><sub>2</sub></font>/(2K)</font>
          </div> </td> 
        </tr> 
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p> Optimal Run Size Q* = {(2C<font><sub>1</sub></font>xK)/[C<font><sub>2</sub></font>(K - x)] }<font><sup>1/2</sup></font><br> Run Length = Q*/K<br> Depletion Length = Q*(K-x)/(xK)<br> Optimal Cycle T* = {(2C<font><sub>1</sub></font>)/[C<font><sub>2</sub></font>x(1 - x/K)] }<font><sup>1/2</sup></font><br> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C">Numerical Example 3:</font> Suppose the demand for a certain energy saving device is x = 1800 units per year (or 6 units each day, assuming 300 working days in a year). The company can produce at an annual rate of K = 7200 units (or 24 per day). Set up cost C<font><sub>1</sub></font> = $300. There is an inventory holding cost C<font><sub>2</sub></font> = $36 per unit, per year. The problem is to find the optimal run size, Q. </p>
      <p> Q* = 200 units per production run. The optimal production cycle is 200/7200 = 0.0278 years, that is 8 and 1/3 of a day. Number of cycle per year is 1800/200 = 9 cycles. </p>
      <p> <br> </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Production and Consumption with Shortages:</b></font> Suppose shortages are permitted at a backorder cost C4 per unit, per time period. A cycle will now look like the following figure: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <img src="graphprod.gif"> 
      </center> 
      <p> If we permit shortages, the peak shortage occurs when production commences at the beginning of a cycle. It can be shown that: </p>
      <p> Optimal Production = q* = {[(2C<font><sub>1</sub></font>x)/C<font><sub>2</sub></font>][K/(K- x)][(C<font><sub>2</sub></font>+C<font><sub>4</sub></font>)/C<font><sub>4</sub></font>]}<font><sup>1/2</sup></font> </p>
      <p> Period per Cycle Is: T = q/x </p>
      <p> Optimal Inventory Is: Q* = t<font><sub>2</sub></font>(K-x) </p>
      <p> Optimal Shortage Is: P* = t<font><sub>1</sub></font>(K-x); </p>
      <p> Total Cost Is: TC = {[(C<font><sub>2</sub></font>t<font><sub>2</sub></font><font><sup>2</sup></font> + C<font><sub>4</sub></font>t<font><sub>1</sub></font><font><sup>2</sup></font>)(K-x)] + [(2C<font><sub>1</sub></font>x)/K] }/ {2(t<font><sub>1</sub></font>+t<font><sub>2</sub></font>)}, </p>
      <p> where, </p>
      <p> t<font><sub>1</sub></font> = {[2xC<font><sub>1</sub></font>C<font><sub>2</sub></font>]/[C<font><sub>4</sub></font>K(K-x)(C<font><sub>2</sub></font>+C<font><sub>4</sub></font>)]}<font><sup>1/2</sup></font>, </p>
      <p> and </p>
      <p> t<font><sub>2</sub></font> = {[2xC<font><sub>1</sub></font>C<font><sub>4</sub></font>]/[C<font><sub>2</sub></font>K(K-x)(C<font><sub>2</sub></font>+C<font><sub>4</sub></font>)]}<font><sup>1/2</sup></font> </p>
      <p> <br> You may like using <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Inventory.htm" target="new">Inventory Control Models</a> JavaScript for checking your computation. You may also perform sensitivity analysis by means of some numerical experimentation for a deeper understanding of the managerial implications in dealing with uncertainties of the parameters of each model </p>
      <p> <b>Further Reading:</b><br> <font size="-2" face="Bookman Old Style"> Zipkin P., <i>Foundations of Inventory Management</i>, McGraw-Hill, 2000. </font> </p>
      <p> <a name="rdiscountes"> </a></p>
      <hr>
      <a name="rdiscountes"> </a> 
      <h3><font color="#DC143C">Optimal Order Quantity Discounts</font></h3> The solution procedure for determination of the optimal order quantity under discounts is as follows: 
      <ul> 
       <li>Step 1: Compute Q for the unit cost associated with each discount category. <p> </p></li>
       <li>Step 2: For those Q that are too small to receive the discount price, adjust the order quantity upward to the nearest quantity that will receive the discount price. <p> </p></li>
       <li>Step 3: For each order quantity determined from Steps 1 and 2, compute the total annual inventory price using the unit price associated with that quantity. The quantity that yields the lowest total annual inventory cost is the optimal order quantity. </li>
      </ul> 
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Quantity Discount Application:</b></font> Suppose the total demand for an expensive electronic machine is 200 units, with ordering cost of $2500, and holding cost of $190, together with the following discount price offering: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center>
       <table border="3"> 
        <tbody>
         <tr>
          <th align="center">Order Size</th>
          <th align="center">Price</th>
         </tr>
         <tr> 
          <th align="center">1-49</th>
          <th align="center">$1400</th>
         </tr>
         <tr> 
          <th align="center">50-89</th>
          <th align="center">$1100</th>
         </tr>
         <tr> 
          <th align="center">90+</th>
          <th align="center">$900</th>
         </tr>
         <tr> 
         </tr>
        </tbody>
       </table>
      </center> 
      <p> The Optimal Ordering Quantity:<br> </p>
      <center>
       Q* = (2xC1/C2) 
       <sup>1/2</sup> = [ 2(2500)(200)/190] 
       <sup>1/2</sup> = 72.5 units.
       <br> 
       <p>The total cost is = [(2500)(200)/72.5] + [(190)(72.5)/2] + [(1100)(200)] = $233784</p>
      </center> 
      <p> The total cost for ordering quantity Q = 90 units is:</p>
      <p></p>
      <center>
        TC(90) = [(2500)(200)/90] + [(190)(90)/2] + [(900)(200)] = $233784,
      </center> 
      <p>this is the lowest total cost order quantity. </p>
      <p>Therefore, should order Q = 90 units </p>
      <p> <br> You may like using <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Inventory.htm" target="new">Inventory Control Models</a> JavaScript for checking your computation. You may also perform sensitivity analysis by means of some numerical experimentation for a deeper understanding of the managerial implications in dealing with uncertainties of the parameters in the model </p>
      <p> <b>Further Reading:</b><br> <font size="-2" face="Bookman Old Style"> Zipkin P., <i>Foundations of Inventory Management</i>, McGraw-Hill, 2000. </font> </p>
      <p> <a name="rfiniteplans"> </a></p>
      <hr>
      <a name="rfiniteplans"> </a> 
      <h3><font color="#DC143C"> Finite Planning Horizon Inventory </font></h3> 
     </dd>
     <dd>
       We already know from our analysis of the "Simple EOQ" approach that any fixed lot size will create "leftovers" which increase total cost unnecessarily. A better approach is to order "whole periods worth" of stock. But the question is should you order one (period worth), or two, or more? As usual, it depends. At first, increasing the buy quantity saves money because order costs are reduced since fewer buys are made. Eventually, though, large order quantities will begin to increase total costs as holding costs rise. 
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>The Silver-Meal Method:</b></font> The Silver-Meal Algorithm trades-off ordering and holding costs by analyzing the problem "one buy at a time". The idea is should the first buy cover period 1, periods 1 and 2, periods 1, 2, and 3, and so forth. To answer this question, the procedure considers each potential buy quantity sequentially and calculates the "average cost per period covered" as the sum of the ordering and holding costs implied by the potential buy divided by the number of periods which would be covered by such an order. Simply put, the decision rule is: "Add the next period's demand to the current order quantity unless the average cost per period covered would not be reduced, that is, as long as the average cost per period covered by the order would be reduced by adding an additional period worth to the order, we will do so. If adding an additional period worth to the order would not reduce the average cost per period covered, then we will consider that the order size is determined, and we will begin to calculate the next order using the same procedure. We will continue one order at a time until every period has been covered with an order. </p>
      <p> <b>Silver-Meal Logic:</b> Increase T, the number of periods covered by next replenishment order, until the total relevant costs per period (over the periods covered by the order) start to decrease. The Silver-Meal method is a "near optimal" heuristic which builds order quantities by taking a marginal analysis approach. Start with the first period in which an order is required. Calculate the average per-period cost of ordering for the next t periods: AC<sub>i</sub>, i = 1, 2, ... Select the smallest i* that satisfies AC<sub>i*</sub> &lt; AC<sub>i*+1</sub> </p>
      <p> Notice that this method assumes that AC<sub>i</sub>/i initially decreases then increases, and never decreases again as t increases, but this is not always true. Moreover, solution is myopic so it may leave only one, two, or a few periods for the final batch, even if the setup cost is high. However, Extensive numerical studies show that the results are usually within 1 or 2 percent of optimal (using mixed-integer linear programming) if horizon is not extremely short. </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Finite Planning Horizon Inventory Application:</b></font> Suppose the forecasted demand for a row material in a manufacturing process for the beginning of the next twelve periods is: </p>

      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Wagner and Whitin Approach:</b></font> It is a considerably more laborious procedure than Silver-Meal which is based on the principles of dynamic programming. </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Mixed Integer Linear Programming:</b></font> The Finite Planning Horizon Inventory decision can be formulated and solved exactly as an integer program. Zero-one integer variables are introduced to accommodate the ordering costs. Decision Variables are: quantity purchased in period i , buy variable = 1 if Qi is positive, = 0 o.w., Beginning inventory for period i, Ending inventory for period i. The objective is to minimize the total overall costs, subject to mixed-integer linear constraints. </p>
      <p> Formulating the above application as an mixed-integer linear program, the optimal solution is: </p>
      <p> Order 550 at the beginning of period 1 <br> Order 450 at the beginning of period 6 <br> Order 450 at the beginning of period 9 <br> Order 550 at the beginning of period 11 <br> The optimal total cost is $3750. </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Conclusions:</b></font> Optimal solutions trade-off ordering and holding costs across time periods based on the certainty of the demand schedule. In practice, the procedure would be re-run each month, with a new month added on the end, and the old month eliminated. Only the most immediate orders would be placed; the later orders would be held. In this sort of "rolling horizon" application, short-term look-ahead procedures like Silver-Meal typically can out-perform the "optimal" approaches, particularly if updates are made to demand forecasts within the planning horizon. </p>
      <p> <b>Further Reading:</b><br> <font size="-2" face="Bookman Old Style"> Zipkin P., <i>Foundations of Inventory Management</i>, McGraw-Hill, 2000. </font> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <hr> 
      <p> <a name="rinventnews"> </a></p>
      <h3><a name="rinventnews"><font color="#DC143C">Inventory Control with Uncertain Demand</font></a></h3>
      <a name="rinventnews"> </a> Suppose you are selling a perishable item (e.g., flower bunches in a florist shop) having random demands X. Your decision under uncertainty is mainly the following question: How many should I order to maximize my profit? 
      <p>Your profit is: </p>
      <ul> 
       <li>P <font face="symbol">´</font> X - (D-X) <font face="symbol">´</font> L, &nbsp; &nbsp;for any X less than D, and <br> </li>
       <li>P <font face="symbol">´</font> D, &nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;for any X at least equal to D </li>
      </ul>where D is the daily order, P is your unit profit, and L is the loss for any left over item. 
      <p>It can be shown that the optimal ordering quantity D* with the largest expected daily profit is a function of the Empirical Cumulative Distribution Function (ECDF) = F(x). More specifically, the optimal quantity is X* where F(x) either equals or exceeds the ratio P/(P + L) for the first time. </p>
      <p>The following numerical example illustrates the process. Given P = $20, L = $10, suppose you have taken records of the past frequency of the demand D over a period of time. Based on this information one can construct the following table. </p>
      <p></p>
      <center>
       <font color="#dc143c" size="3"><b>Optimal Ordering Quantity</b></font>
       <table border="3"> 
        <tbody> 
         <tr> 
          <th>Demand<br>Daily (x)</th>
          <th>Relative<br>Frequency </th>
          <th>ECDF </th>
          <th>Expected<br>Profit</th>
         </tr>
         <tr>

      </center>
      <p>The critical ratio P/(P + L) = 20/30 = 0.6667, indicating D* = X* = 6 units. </p>
      <p>To verify this decision, one may use the following recursive formula in computing: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center>
       Expected profit [D+1] = Expected profit [D] - (P + L)F(x) + P 
      </center>
      <p>The daily expected profit using this formula computed and recorded in the last column of the above table with the optimal daily profit is $75.20. </p>
      <p> You may like using <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Newsboy.htm" target="new">Single-period Inventory Analysis</a> JavaScript to check your computation. </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> <b>Further Reading:</b><br> <font size="-2" face="Bookman Old Style"> Silver E., D. Pyke, and R. Peterson, <i>Inventory Management and Production Planning and Scheduling</i>, Wiley, 1998. </font> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <hr> 
      <p> <a name="rinvenmanage"> </a></p>
      <h3><a name="rinvenmanage"><font color="#DC143C">Managing and Controlling Inventory </font></a></h3>
      <a name="rinvenmanage"> </a> 
     </dd>
     <dd>
      <p> Inventory models give answers to two questions. When should an order be placed or a new lot be manufactured? And how much should be ordered or purchased? </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Inventories are held for the following reasons:</b></font> </p>
      <ul> 
       <li>To meet anticipated customer demand with large fluctuations. </li>
       <li>To protect against shortages. </li>
       <li>To take advantage quantity discounts. </li>
       <li>To maintain independence of operations. </li>
       <li>To smooth production requirements. </li>
       <li>To guard against price increases. </li>
       <li>To take advantage of order cycles. </li>
       <li>To overcome the variations in delivery times. </li>
       <li>To guard against uncertain production schedules. </li>
       <li>To count for the possibility of large number of defects. </li>
       <li>To guard against poor forecasts of customer demand. </li>
      </ul> 
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>A Factors-Guideline for Developing a "good" Inventory System</b></font> </p>
      <ul> 
       <li>A system to keep track of inventory by reviewing continuously or periodically. </li>
       <li>A reliable forecast of demand. </li>
       <li>Reasonable estimates of:
        <ul> 
         <li>Holding costs </li>
         <li>Ordering costs </li>
         <li>Shortage costs </li>
         <li>Lead Time </li>
        </ul> </li>
       <li>Interest on loans to purchase inventory or opportunity costs because of funds tied up in inventory. </li>
       <li>Taxes, and insurance costs. </li>
       <li>Ordering and setup costs. </li>
       <li>Costs of holding an item in inventory. </li>
       <li>Cost of funds tied up in inventory. </li>
       <li>Transportation &amp; shipping cost. </li>
       <li>Receiving and inspection costs. </li>
       <li>Handling &amp; storage cost. </li>
       <li>Accounting and auditing cost. </li>
       <li>Storage costs such as rent, heating, lighting, and security. </li>
       <li>Depreciation cost. </li>
       <li>Obsolescence cost. </li>
      </ul> 
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>How to Reduce the Inventory Costs?</b></font> </p>
      <ul> 
       <li>Cycle inventory. </li>
       <li>Streamline ordering/production process. </li>
       <li>Increase repeatability. </li>
       <li>Safety Stock inventory. </li>
       <li>Better timing of orders. </li>
       <li>Improve forecasts. </li>
       <li>Reduce supply uncertainties. </li>
       <li>Use capacity cushions instead. </li>
       <li>Anticipation inventory. </li>
       <li>Match production rate with demand rate. </li>
       <li>Use complementary products. </li>
       <li>Off-season promotions. </li>
       <li>Creative pricing. </li>
       <li>Pipeline inventory. </li>
       <li>Reduce lead time. </li>
       <li>More responsive suppliers. </li>
       <li>Decrease lot size when it affects lead times. </li>
      </ul> 
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>The ABC Classification</b></font> The ABC classification system is to grouping items according to annual sales volume, in an attempt to identify the small number of items that will account for most of the sales volume and that are the most important ones to control for effective inventory management. </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Types of Inventory Control Reviews:</b></font> The inventory level for different products can be monitored either continuously or on a periodic basis. </p>
      <ul> 
       <li>Continuous review systems: Each time a withdrawal is made from inventory, the remaining quantity of the item is reviewed to determine whether an order should be placed <p> </p></li>
       <li>Periodic review systems: The inventory of an item is reviewed at fixed time intervals, and an order Is placed for the appropriate amount </li>
      </ul> 
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Advantage and Disadvantage of Fixed-Period Model:</b></font> </p>
      <ul> 
       <li>Do not have to continuously monitor inventory levels. </li>
       <li>Does not require computerized inventory system. </li>
       <li>Low cost of maintenance. </li>
       <li>Higher inventory carrying cost. </li>
       <li>Orders placed at fixed intervals. </li>
       <li>Inventory brought up to target amount. </li>
       <li>Amounts ordered may vary. </li>
       <li>No continuous inventory count is needed; however there is a possibility of being out of stock between intervals. </li>
       <li>Useful when lead time is very short. </li>
      </ul> 
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Cash Flow and Forecasting:</b></font> Balance sheets and profit and loss statements indicate the health of your business at the end of the financial year. What they fail to show you is the timing of payments and receipts and the importance of cash flow. </p>
      <p> Your business can survive without cash for a short while but it will need to be "liquid" to pay the bills as and when they arrive. A cash flow statement, usually constructed over the course of a year, compares your cash position at the end of the year to the position at the start, and the constant flow of money into and out of the business over the course of that year. </p>
      <p> The amount your business owes and is owed is covered in the profit and loss statement; a cash flow statement deals only with the money circulating in the business. It is a useful tool in establishing whether your business is eating up the cash or generating the cash. </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Working Capital Cycle:</b></font> Cash flows in a cycle into, around and out of a business. It is the business's life blood and every manager's primary task is to help keep it flowing and to use the cash flow to generate profits. If a business is operating profitably, then it should, in theory, generate cash surpluses. If it doesn't generate surpluses, the business will eventually run out of cash and expire. </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <img src="CashFlow.Gif"> 
      </center> 
      <p> Each component of working capital, namely inventory, receivable and payable has two dimensions, time, and money. When it comes to managing working capital -- Time is money. If you can get money to move faster around the cycle, e.g. collect moneys due from debtors more quickly or reduce the amount of money tied up, e.g. reduce inventory levels relative to sales, the business will generate more cash or it will need to borrow less money to fund working capital. As a consequence, you could reduce the cost of interest or you will have additional money available to support additional sales growth. Similarly, if you can negotiate improved terms with suppliers e.g. get longer credit or an increased credit limit, you effectively create free finance to help fund future sales. </p>
      <p> The following are some of the main factors in managing a ?good? cash flow system: </p>
      <ul> 
       <li> If you collect receivable (debtors) faster then you release cash from the cycle </li>
       <li> If you collect receivable slower, then your receivable soak up cash </li>
       <li> If you get better credit, in terms of duration or amount from suppliers then you increase your cash resources. </li>
       <li> If you sift inventory faster then you free up cash. If you move inventory slower then you consume more cash. </li>
      </ul> 
      <p> <b>Further Reading:</b><br> <font size="-2" face="Bookman Old Style"> Schaeffer H., <i>Essentials of Cash Flow</i>, Wiley, 2002. <br> Silver E., D. Pyke, and R. Peterson, <i>Inventory Management and Production Planning and Scheduling</i>, Wiley, 1998.<br> Simini J., <i>Cash Flow Basics for Nonfinancial Managers</i>, Wiley, 1990. </font> </p>
      <hr> 
      <p> <a name="rimpressionss"> </a></p>
      <h3><a name="rimpressionss"><font color="#DC143C">Marketing and Modeling Advertising Campaign</font></a></h3>
      <a name="rimpressionss"> </a> 
      <font color="#DC143C"><b>Introduction:</b></font> A broad classification of mathematical advertising models results in models based on concept of selling with some assumed advertising/sales response functions and those based on marketing using the theory of consumer buying behavior. 
      <p> <b>The Evolution of Marketing</b> </p>
      <p> <b>Production Orientation:</b> Utilities having a production orientation would view their only responsibility to the customer as ensuring that product (electric or gas) is available to the customer when needed. Regulators set prices after public hearings and a regulatory process established within the state systems, and bills are rendered to customers based upon consumption. </p>
      <p> <b>Sales Orientation:</b> Utilities having excess capacity of either electric or gas would focus efforts on enticing customers to buy more of their products without specific regard for how customers may need or use the products, making sales orientation dominant. </p>
      <p> <b>Marketing Orientation:</b> What is meant by a "marketing orientation?" essentially, it calls for every employee in a firm to focus on satisfying the wants and needs of the customer; it claims that customers do not so much buy a product or service as seek to have their wants and need satisfied and that firms exist to produce satisfied customers. Consistent with this is the construct that the customer, not the firm, determines value. </p>
      <p> <a name="radsensusM"> </a></p>
      <hr>
      <a name="radsensusM"> </a> 
      <h3><font color="#DC143C">Selling Models</font></h3> Selling focuses on the needs of seller. Selling models are concerned with the sellers need to convert the product into cash. One of the most well known selling models is the advertising/sales response model (ASR) that assumes the shape of the relationship between sales and advertising is known. 
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>The Vidale and Wolfe Model:</b></font> Vidale and Wolfe developed a single-equation model of sales response to advertising based on experimental studies of advertising effectiveness. This sales behavior through time relative to different levels of advertising expenditure for a firm, consistent with their empirical observation, has been developed. </p>
      <p> The three parameters in this model are: </p>
      <ul> 
       <li>The sales decay constant (<font face="Symbol">l</font>): the sales decay constant is defined as the rate at which sales of the product decrease in the absence of advertising. </li>
       <li>The saturation level (m): the saturation level of a product is defined as the practical limit of sales that can be captured by the product. </li>
       <li>The sales response constant (r): the sales response constant is defined as the addition to sales per round of advertising when sales are zero. </li>
      </ul> 
      <p> The fundamental assumptions in this model are as follows: </p>
      <ol> 
       <li> Sales would decrease in the absence of advertising; </li>
       <li>The existence of a saturation level beyond which sales would not increase; </li>
       <li>The rate of response to advertising is constant per dollar spent; </li>
       <li>The advertising is assumed to be operative only on those potential customers who are not customers at the present time; i.e., advertising merely obtains new customers and does not make existing customers increase their volume of purchase; </li>
       <li>The effectiveness of different media is negligible over one another; </li>
       <li> On the basis of empirical evidence, it is assumed that the sales phenomenon can be represented mathematically by the relation: <p> </p>
        <center>
          dS/dt = r A(t)[(m-S)/m - 
         <font face="Symbol">l</font> S(t) 
         <p> </p>
        </center> <p> where dS/dt is the instantaneous change in the rate of sales at time t, S is the rate of sales at time t, A(t) is the rate of advertising at time t, r is the sales response constant, <font face="Symbol">l</font> is the sales decay constant and m is the saturation level of sales. </p></li>
      </ol> This equation suggests that the change or increase in the rate of sales will be greater the higher the sales response constant; the lower the sales decay constant 
      <font face="Symbol">l</font>, the higher the saturation level, and the higher the advertising expenditure. 
      <p> The three parameters r, <font face="Symbol">l</font>, and m are constant for a given product and campaign. </p>
      <p>The sales response, r, is assessed by measuring the increase in the rate of sales resulting from a given amount of advertising in a test area with controlled conditions. The sales decay constant <font face="Symbol">l</font>, is assessed by measuring the decline in sales in a test area when advertising is reduced to zero. The saturation level of sales, m, is assessed from market research information on the size of the total market. Note that the term (m-S)/S is the sales potential remaining in the market which can be captured by advertising campaigns. </p>
      <p>The model can be rearranged and written as: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center>
        dS/dt + [r A(t)/m + 
       <font face="Symbol">l</font>)] S(t) = r A(t) 
       <p> </p>
      </center> 
      <p> The following depict a typical sales response to an advertising campaign. </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <a href="sale2.gif"><img alt="A Typical Sales Response" src="sale2.gif" width="174" height="189" border="0"></a> 
       <br>
       <p>A Typical Sales Response<br> <b><font color="#dc143c">Click on the image to enlarge it and THEN print it</font></b> </p>
      </center> 
      <p> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <a href="sale1.gif"><img alt="A Typical Advertising Campaign" src="sale1.gif" width="174" height="189" border="0"></a> 
       <br>
       <p>A Typical Advertising Campaign<br> <b><font color="#dc143c">Click on the image to enlarge it and THEN print it</font></b> </p>
      </center> 
      <p> </p>
      <p> The advertising campaign has a constant rate A(t) =A of advertising expenditure maintained for duration T, after which A is almost zero: </p>
      <p> &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; <font face="symbol">æ</font> A&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp; for 0 <font face="Symbol">£</font> t <font face="Symbol">£</font> T,<br> A(t) = &nbsp; <font face="symbol">ç</font><br> &nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; <font face="symbol">è</font> 0&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp; for t <font face="Symbol">&gt;</font>T </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> While many marketing researchers have aligned the ASR approach as an established school in advertising modeling, nevertheless they readily admit the most aggravating problem is the assumption on the shape of the ASR function. Moreover, ASR models do not consider the need and motives leading to consumer behavior. It is well established that marketing managers are concerned about delivering product benefit, changing brand attitudes, and influencing consumer perceptions. Marketing management realizes that advertising plans must be based on the psychological and social forces that condition consumer behavior; that is, what goes on inside the consumer's head. </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> <a name="radecoTimemodel"> </a></p>
      <hr>
      <a name="radecoTimemodel"> </a> 
      <h3><font color="#DC143C">Buying Models</font></h3> Modern business firms have oriented their advertising campaigns into a fully consumer buying behavior approach rather than selling. This approach is based on the marketing wisdom: in order to sell something the marketer must know what the potential buyer wants and/or wants to hear. There has been considerable discussion in marketing literature about "consumer behavior". This discussion centers around the need for marketing to be consumer-oriented, to be concerned with the idea of satisfying the needs of the consumer by means of the product and the whole cluster of factors associated with creating, delivering, and finally consuming it. This is now possible by considering the needed technological advances such as "brain-storming". 
      <p> <b>Modeling Consumer Choice:</b> When the modular and the decision maker come up with a good model of customer choice among discrete options, they often implement their model of customer choice. However, one might take the advantage of using multi-method object -oriented software (e.g., AnyLogic ) that the practical problem can be modeled at multiple levels of aggregation, where, e.g., the multi-nominal logit of discrete choice methods are represented by object state-chart transitions (e.g. from "aware" state to "buy" state) -- the transition is the custom probability function estimated by the discrete choice method. Multi-level objects representing subgroups easily represent nesting. Moreover, each object can have multiple state-charts. </p>
      <p> The consumer buying behavior approach to advertising modeling presumes that advertising influences sales by altering the taste, preference and attitude of the consumer, and the firm's effort in communication that results in a purchase. </p>
      <p> Since there are a multitude of social-psychological factors affecting buying behavior, some of them complex and unknown to the advertiser, it is preferable to consider the probabilistic version of consumer buying behavior model. Because of the diminishing effect of advertising, often an advertising pulsing policy as opposed to the constant policy may increase the effectiveness of advertising, especially on the impact of repetition in advertising. </p>
      <p> The operational model with additional characteristics is often derived by optimal advertising strategy over a finite advertising duration campaign. The prescribed strategies are the maximizer of a discounted profit function which includes the firm's attitude toward uncertainty in sales. The needed operational issues, such as estimation of parameters and self-validating, are also recommended. </p>
      <p> The marketing literature provides strong evidence that consumers do substitute rules of their own for information about product quality, perceived value, and price. The lower search costs associated with the rules, for example, may more than offset the monetary or quality losses. Generally, consumers tend to perceive heavily advertised brands to be of higher quality. The psychological studies have discovered that human-being is an "attitudinal being" and evaluates just about everything they come into contact with through "revision of all values". Consider the question "How do you feel abut this particular brand?" Surely, the answer depends on the degree to which you like or dislike, value or disvalue, the brand. The crux of the consumer behavior model then is that the marketer attempt to recognize consumer as an attitudinal being who constantly revises all values, even within a given segment. Once the goal-directed behavior is manifested, the consumer experiences the consequences of his or her behavior. He or she uses this experience as a source of learning in which he or she revises his or her total attitude toward the product or service. Several researchers have expressed the fact that attitude alone determines subsequent behavior. </p>
      <p> A summary flow chart of a simple model is shown in the following figure: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <a href="consumer.gif"><img alt="Consumer Behavior" src="consumer.gif" width="174" height="189" border="0"></a> 
       <br>
       <p>Consumer Behavior<br> <b><font color="#dc143c">Click on the image to enlarge it and THEN print it</font></b> </p>
      </center> 
      <p> </p>
      <p> The structure of the decision process of a typical consumer concerning a specific brand X, contains three functional values namely attitude A(t), level of buying B(t) and communication C(t). </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Nicosia's Model: </b></font> The Nicosia model's dynamic state equations are described by the following two linear algebraic/differential equations: </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center>
        B
       <font face="symbol">¢</font>(t) = dB(t)/dt = b[A(t) - 
       <font face="Symbol">b</font>B(t)] 
       <p> A<font face="symbol">¢</font>(t) = dA(t)/dt = a[B(t) - <font face="Symbol">a</font>A(t)] + C(t) </p>
       <p> </p>
      </center> 
      <p> Where: </p>
      <p> B(t) = the Buying behavior; i.e., purchase rate at time t. </p>
      <p> A(t) = The consumers' Attitude toward the brand which results from some variety of complex interactions of various factors, some of which are indicated in the above Figure. </p>
      <p> C(t) = The impact of communication (advertising campaign) made by the business firm. This may be any stimuli, a new package design or in general an advertisement of a particular brand. A successful marketing strategy is to develop product and promotional stimuli that consumers will perceive as relevant to their needs. Consumer needs are also influenced by factors such as consumer past experience and social influences. Because of the diminishing effect of advertising, we may consider C(t) to be a pulse function, as opposed to the constant advertising policy. </p>
      <p> <font face="symbol">a</font>, <font face="symbol">b</font>, a, and b are the 'personality' parameters of the equations of the model. These parameters are assumed to be constant with respect to time. </p>
      <p> The main major drawbacks of the above descriptive models are: </p>
      <p> 1) That the advertising rate is constant over time. It is clear that the return on constant advertising is diminishing with time and hence it is not related to the volume of sales; therefore further expenditures on advertising will not bring abut any substantial increase in the sales revenues. The term "advertising modeling" has been used to describe the decision process of improving sales of a product or a service. A substantial expense in marketing is advertising expenses. The effect of repetitions of a stimulus on the consumer's ability to recall the message is a major issue in learning theory. It is well established that advertising must be continuous to stop it being forgotten. </p>
      <p> </p>
      <p> <a name="radSimulEqns"> </a></p>
      <hr>
      <a name="radSimulEqns"> </a> 
      <h3><font color="#DC143C">The Advertising Pulsing Policy</font></h3> The advertising pulsing policy (APP) is a policy with a high constant advertising intensity, alternating with periods with no advertising, as shown in the following figure: 
      <p> </p>
      <p> </p>
      <center> 
       <a href="PULSING.GIF"><img alt="Advertising Pulsing Policy" src="PULSING.GIF" width="174" height="189" border="0"></a> 
       <br>
       <p>Advertising Pulsing Policy<br> <b><font color="#dc143c">Click on the image to enlarge it and THEN print it</font></b> </p>
      </center> 
      <p> APP may be preferable to one of constant advertising over the campaign duration. </p>
      <p> 2) That the advertising horizon is an infinite time. This infinite horizon decreases the models' use since budget planning for advertising expenditures seldom has an infinite horizon. However, in the Nicosia's model it is not clear how to generate the sales response function when advertising is discontinued. </p>
      <p> <br> </p>
      <p> <a name="radSatatistErrors"> </a></p>
      <hr>
      <a name="radSatatistErrors"> </a> 
      <h3><font color="#DC143C">Internet Advertising</font></h3> 
      <p> It is a fact of business that in order to make money, you have to spend it first. For most business it is the spending on advertising. And for the online business, there is no shortage of options to choose from. </p>
      <p> Most websites offer some kind of graphic or text advertising, and there are a bewildering variety of mailing lists, newsletters, and regular mailings. However, before deciding where to advertise, one must think of why advertising? </p>
      <p> For many companies the aim of an advert is to increase sales to make more money. For others, it might be increase in profile, increasing brand awareness, and testing new pricing strategies or new markets. Therefore, it is necessary to know exactly what it is to be achieved. This determines where to advertise.</p>
      <p> When selecting a site to advertise, the main factor is to ask how large the targeted audience is and the price to pay for. The price could a flat fee, a cost-per-click, pay per exposure, or some other arrangement including the cost of a professional designer to create and maintain the ad, and the duration of campaign. </p>
      <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Banner Advertising:</b></font> If you have spent any time surfing the Internet, you have seen more than your fair share of <b>banner ads</b>. These small rectangular advertisements appear on all sorts of Web pages and vary considerably in appearance and subject matter, but they all share a basic function: if you click on them, your Internet browser will take you to the advertiser's Web site. </p>
      <p> Over the past few years, most of us have heard about all the money being made on the Internet. This new medium of education and entertainment has revolutionized the economy and brought many people and many companies a great deal of success. But where is all this money coming from? There are a lot of ways Web sites make money, but one of the main sources of revenue is advertising. And one of the most popular forms of Internet advertising is the banner ad. </p>
      <p>Because of its graphic element, a banner ad is somewhat similar to a traditional ad you would see in a printed publication such as a newspaper or magazine, but it has the added ability to bring a potential customer directly to the advertiser's Web site. This is something like touching a printed ad and being immediately contacted the advertiser's store! A banner ad also differs from a print ad in its dynamic capability. It stays in one place on a page, like a magazine ad, but it can present multiple images, include animation and change appearance in a number of other ways. Different measures are more important to different advertisers, but most advertisers consider all of these elements when judging the effectiveness of a banner ad. <b>Cost per sale</b> is the measure of how much advertising money is spent on making one sale. Advertisers use different means to calculate this, depending on the ad and the product or service. Many advertisers keep track of visitor activity using <b>Internet cookies</b> . This technology allows the site to combine shopping history with information about how the visitor originally came to the site. <script>

The main factors are the total cost, the cost per thousand impressions (CPM), and number of ads shown, i.e., the exposures.
