Using the <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Regression.htm" target="new"><font color="#dc143c"><b>Regression Analysis</b></font></a> JavaScript, for the above data, we obtain: </p><p> b = Slope = -0.32, y-Intercept = log(k) = 1.08 </p><p> log y = log(k) + b log(t) </p><p> b = -0.32 </p><p> k = 10<sup>1.08</sup> = 12 </p><p> y(t) = 12 t <sup>-0.32</sup> </p><p> r = 2<sup>b</sup> = 2<sup>-0.32</sup> = 80% </p><p> <b>Conclusions:</b> As expected while number of cycles doubles, cycle time decreases by a constant %, that is, the result is a 20% decrease or 80% learning ratio or 80% learning curve with a mathematical model y(t) = 12 t <sup>-0.32</sup> </p><p> <b>Further Readings:</b><br> <font size="-2" face="Bookman Old Style"> Dilworth J., <i>Production and Operations Management: Manufacturing and Non-manufacturing</i>, Random House Business Division, 2003.<br> Krajewski L., and L. Ritzman, <i>Operations Management: Strategy and Analysis</i>, Addison-Wesley Publishing Company, 2004.<br> </font> </p><p> </p><p> <a name="rIndeces"> </a></p>
        <hr><a name="rIndeces"> </a> <h3><font color="#DC143C">Economics and Financial Ratios and Price Indices</font></h3> Economics and finance use and analysis ratios for comparison and as a measuring tool and decision process for the purpose of evaluating certain aspects of company's operations. The following are among the widely used ratios: <p> <b>Liquidity Ratios:</b> Liquidity ratios measure a firm's ability to meet its current obligations, for example: </p>
        <ul> 
         <li>Acid Test or Quick Ratio = (Cash + Marketable Securities + Accounts Receivable) / Current Liabilities <p></p></li>
         <li> Cash Ratio = (Cash Equivalents + Marketable Securities) / Current Liabilities </li>
        </ul> <p> <b>Profitability Ratios:</b> Profitability ratios profitability ratios measure management's ability to control expenses and to earn a return on the resources committed to the business, for example: </p>
        <ul>
         <li> Operating Income Margin = Operating Income / Net Sales <p></p></li>
         <li> Gross Profit Margin = Gross Profit / Net Sales </li>
        </ul> <p> <b>Leverage Ratios:</b> Leverage ratios measure the degree of protection of suppliers of long-term funds and can also aid in judging a firm's ability to raise additional debt and its capacity to pay its liabilities on time, for example: </p>
        <ul>
         <li> Total Debts to Assets = Total Liabilities / Total Assets <p></p></li>
         <li> Capitalization Ratio= Long-Term Debt /(Long-Term Debt + Owners' Equity) </li>
        </ul> <p> <b>Efficiency:</b> Efficiency activity or turnover ratios provide information about management's ability to control expenses and to earn a return on the resources committed to the business, for example: </p>
        <ul>
         <li> Cash Turnover = Net Sales / Cash <p></p></li>
         <li> Inventory Turnover = Cost of Goods Sold / Average Inventory </li>
        </ul> <p> <font color="#DC143C"><b>Price Indices</b></font> </p><p> </p>
        <dd>
         Index numbers are used when one is trying to compare series of numbers of vastly different size. It is a way to standardize the measurement of numbers so that they are directly comparable. 
         <p> </p>
         <p> The simplest and widely used measure of inflation is the Consumer Price Index (CPI). To compute the price index, the cost of the market basket in any period is divided by the cost of the market basket in the base period, and the result is multiplied by 100. </p>
         <p> If you want to forecast the economic future, you can do so without knowing anything about how the economy works. Further, your forecasts may turn out to be as good as those of professional economists. The key to your success will be the Leading Indicators, an index of items that generally swing up or down before the economy as a whole does. </p>
         <p> </p>

         <p> we found that using period 1 quantity, the price index in period 2 is</p> 
         <p>($4.39/$4.25) x 100 = 103.29</p> 
         <p>Using period 2 quantities, the price index in period 2 is </p> 
         <p>($4.31/$4.35) x 100 = 99.08</p> 
         <p>A better price index could be found by taking the geometric mean of the two. To find the geometric mean, multiply the two together and then take the square root. The result is called a Fisher Index. </p>
         <p> In USA, since January 1999, the geometric mean formula has been used to calculate most basic indexes within the Comsumer Price Indeces (CPI); in other words, the prices within most item categories (e.g., apples) are averaged using a geometric mean formula. This improvement moves the CPI somewhat closer to a cost-of-living measure, as the geometric mean formula allows for a modest amount of consumer substitution as relative prices within item categories change. </p>
         <p> Notice that, since the geometric mean formula is used only to average prices within item categories, it does not account for consumer substitution taking place between item categories. For example, if the price of pork increases compared to those of other meats, shoppers might shift their purchases away from pork to beef, poultry, or fish. The CPI formula does not reflect this type of consumer response to changing relative prices. </p>
         <p> The following are some of useful and widely used price indices: </p>
         <p> <br> <b>Geometric Mean Index:</b> </p>
         <p> </p>
         <center>
           G

         </center>

			form.GM.value = " Does not exist due to zero or negative data value"; }
				else {
				if(error0>0) {
					form.GM.value ="0"; }
				else {
					for(i = 0; i<N; i++) { 
			G1 += Math.log(P[i]) - Math.log(P[0]);                   
                        }
		GM = (G1*P[0]*Q[0])/SUM;
var e = 2.7182818284590452353602874712;
		var GM2 = Math.pow(e,GM);
	GM2 = Math.round(10000000*GM2)/10000000;
					form.GM.value = GM2;
		}
        		
				}
				
}
}
}

<!-- done hiding from old browsers -->
        
</script> 
          <!--input form--> </p>
         <center>

          </form> Replace the numerical example values with your own pairs
          <br>of data, and then click on the Calculate button. 
         </center> 
         <p> <br> <b>Harmonic Mean Index:</b> </p>
         <p> </p>
         <center>
           H
          <sub>j</sub> = (
          <font face="Symbol">S</font>V
          <sub>i</sub>) / [(
          <font face="Symbol">S</font>V
          <sub>i</sub> . p
          <sub>j</sub> )/ p
          <sub>i</sub> ],&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; i = 1, 2,..., j, 
         </center> 
         <p> where p<sub>i</sub> is the price per unit in period i, q<sub>n</sub> is the quantity produced in period i, and V <sub>i</sub> = p<sub>i</sub> q<sub>i</sub> the value of the i units, and subscripts 1 indicate the reference period of n periods. </p>
         <p> <br> <b>Laspeyres' Index:</b> </p>
         <p> </p>
         <center>
           L
          <sub>j</sub> = 
          <font face="Symbol">S</font> (p
          <sub>i</sub>q
          <sub>1</sub>) 
          <font face="Symbol">S</font> (p
          <sub>1</sub>q
          <sub>1</sub>), &nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; the first sum is over&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; i = 1, 2,..., j while the second one is over all &nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;i = 1, 2,..., n, 
         </center> 
         <p> where p<sub>i</sub> is the price per unit in period i and q<sub>i</sub> is the quantity produced in period I, and subscripts 1 indicate the reference period of n periods. </p>
         <p> <br> <b>Paasche's Index:</b> </p>
         <p> </p>
         <center>
           P
          <sub>j</sub> = 
          <font face="Symbol">S</font> (p
          <sub>i</sub>q
          <sub>i</sub>) 
          <font face="Symbol">S</font> (p
          <sub>1</sub>q
          <sub>i</sub>),&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; the first sum is over&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; i = 1, 2,..., j while the second one is over all&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp; i = 1, 2,...., n, 
         </center> 
         <p> where p<sub>i</sub> is the price per unit in period i and q<sub>i</sub> is the quantity produced in period I, and subscripts 1 indicate the reference period of n periods. </p>
         <p> <br> <b>Fisher Index:</b> </p>
         <p> </p>
         <center>
           F
          <sub>j</sub> = [Laspeyres' index
          <sub>j</sub> . Paasche's index
          <sub>j</sub>]
          <sup>1/2</sup> 
         </center> 
         <p> <br> For more economics and financial ratios and indices, visit the <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/opre504.htm#rapplIndexnu" target="new">Index Numbers and Ratios with Applications</a> site </p>

         <a name="rtopicaljava"> </a> 
         <h4><font color="#dc143c">JavaScript E-labs Learning Objects </font></h4> This section is a part of the JavaScript 
         <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/scientificCal.htm" target="new">E-labs</a> learning technologies for decision making. 
         <p> Each JavaScript in this collection is deigned to assisting you in <b>performing numerical experimentation</b>, for at least a couple of hours as students do in, e.g. Physics labs. These leaning objects are your statistics e-labs. These serve as <b>learning tools for a deeper understanding</b> of the fundamental statistical concepts and techniques, by asking "what-if" questions. </p>
         <p> <font color="Red">Decision Making in Economics and Finance:</font> </p>
         <ul> 
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/ABClass.htm" target="new">ABC Inventory Classification</a> -- an analysis of a range of items, such as finished products or customers into three "importance" categories: A, B, and C as a basis for a control scheme. This pageconstructs an empirical cumulative distribution function (ECDF) as a measuring tool and decision procedure for the ABC inventory classification. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Inventory.htm" target="new">Inventory Control Models</a> -- Given the costs of holding stock, placing an order, and running short of stock, this page optimizes decision parameters (order point, order quantity, etc.) using four models: Classical, Shortages Permitted , Production &amp; Consumption, Production &amp; Consumption with Shortages. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Replacement.htm" target="new">Optimal Age for Replacement</a> -- Given yearly figures for resale value and running costs, this page calculates the replacement optimal age and average cost. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Newsboy.htm" target="new">Single-period Inventory Analysis</a> -- computes the optimal inventory level over a single cycle, from up-to-28 pairs of (number of possible item to sell, and their associated non-zero probabilities), together with the "not sold unit batch cost", and the "net profit of a batch sold". </li>
         </ul> 
         <font color="Red">Probabilistic Modeling:</font> 
         <ul> 
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/matrix/matrix.htm" target="new">Bayes' Revised Probability</a> -- computes the posterior probabilities to "sharpen" your uncertainties by incorporating an expert judgement's reliability matrix with your prior probability vector. Can accommodate up to nine states of nature. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/ADuncertain.htm" target="new">Decision Making Under Uncertainty</a> -- Enter up-to-6x6 payoff matrix of decision alternatives (choices) by&nbsp;states of nature, along with a coefficient of optimism; the page will calculate Action &amp; Payoff for Pessimism, Optimism, Middle-of-the-Road, Minimize Regret, and Insufficient Reason. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Utility.htm" target="new">Determination of Utility Function</a> -- Takes two monetary values and their known utility, and calculates the utility of another amount, under two different strategies: certain &amp; uncertain. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/DaRisky.htm" target="new">Making Risky Decisions</a> -- Enter up-to-6x6 payoff matrix of decision alternatives (choices) by states of nature, along with subjective estimates of occurrence probability for each states of nature; the page will calculate action &amp; payoff (expected, and for most likely event), min expected regret , return of perfect information, value of perfect information, and efficiency. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/multinomial.htm" target="new">Multinomial Distributions</a> -- for up to 36 probabilities and associated outcomes, calculates expected value, variance, SD, and CV. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/RevisMean.htm" target="new">Revising the Mean and the Variance</a> -- to combine subjectivity and evidence-based estimates. Takes up to 14 pairs of means and variances; calculates combined estimates of mean, variance, and CV. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/InaccracyAssessmet.htm" target="new">Subjective Assessment of Estimates</a>&nbsp;-- (relative precision as a measuring tool for inaccuracy assessment among estimates), tests the claim that at least one estimate is away from the parameter by more than r times (i.e., a relative precision), where r is a subjective positive number less than one. Takes up-to-10 sample estimates, and a subjective relative precision (r&lt;1); the page indicates whether at least one measurement is unacceptable. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/SubjTest.htm" target="new">Subjectivity in Hypothesis Testing</a> -- Takes the profit/loss measure of various correct or incorrect conclusions regarding the hypothesis, along with probabilities&nbsp;of Type I and II errors (alpha &amp; beta), total sampling cost, and subjective estimate of probability that null hypothesis is true; returns the expected net profit. </li>
         </ul>  
         <font color="Red">Time Series Analysis and Forecasting</font> 
         <ul> 
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Autoreg.htm" target="new">Autoregressive Time Series</a> -- tools for the identification, estimation, and forecasting based on autoregressive order obtained from a time series. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Trend.htm" target="new">Detecting Trend &amp; Autocrrelation in Time Series</a>&nbsp;-- Given a set of numbers, this page tests for trend by Sign Test, and for autocorrelation by Durbin-Watson test. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/graph/TimeSeriesPlot.htm" target="new">Plot of a Time Series</a> -- generates a graph of a time series with up to 144 points. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/SeasonalTools.htm">Seasonal Index</a> -- Calculates a set of seasonal index values from a set of values forming a time series. A related page performs a <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/TestSeason.htm" target="new">Test for Seasonality</a> on the index values. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/ForecaSmo.htm" target="new">Forecasting by Smoothing</a> -- Given a set of numbers forming a time series, this page estimates the next number, using Moving Avg &amp; Exponential Smoothing, Weighted Moving Avg, and Double &amp; Triple Exponential Smoothing, &amp;and Holt's method </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Fluctuation.htm" target="new">Runs Test for Random Fluctuations</a> -- in a time series. </li>
          <li> <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/Stationary.htm" target="new">Test for Stationary Time Series</a>&nbsp;-- Given a set of numbers forming a time series, this page calculates the mean &amp; variance of the first &amp; second half, and calculates one-lag-apart &amp; two-lag-apart autocorrelations. A related page: <a href="http://home.ubalt.edu/ntsbarsh/Business-stat/otherapplets/TimeSeriesStat.htm" target="new">Time Series' Statistics</a> calculates these statistics, and also the overall mean &amp; variance, and the first &amp; second partial autocorrelations. </li>
         </ul>
